<li><i>see also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_dialects_of_English" title="List of dialects of English">List of dialects of English</a></i></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">
Teaching</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding-bottom:0.6em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Teaching_English_as_a_second_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Teaching English as a second language">Teaching English as a second language</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Category:English_words_and_phrases" title="Category:English words and phrases">Category:English words and phrases</a></li></ul>
<hr />
<i>Higher category:</i> <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">Language</a></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:English_language" title="Template:English language"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:English_language" title="Template talk:English language"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:English_language&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Canadian English</b> (<b>CanE</b>, <b>CE</b>, <b>en-CA</b>)<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> is the set of <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">varieties</a> of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/2016_Canadian_Census" title="2016 Canadian Census">2016 census</a>, English was the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> of more than 19.4 million <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadians</a>, or 58.1% of the total population; the remainder of the population were native speakers of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_French" title="Canadian French">Canadian French</a> (20.8%) or other languages (21.1%).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> A larger number, 28 million people, reported using English as their dominant language.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Of Canadians outside the province of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, 82% reported speaking English natively, but within Quebec the figure was just 7.7% as most of its residents are native speakers of <a href="/wiki/Quebec_French" title="Quebec French">Quebec French</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gmcdi_a_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmcdi_a-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Canadian English contains major elements of both <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a>, as well as many uniquely Canadian characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-Dollinger_2008_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dollinger_2008-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> While, broadly speaking, Canadian English tends to be closest to American English in terms of linguistic distance,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Labov_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> the precise influence of American English, British English and other sources on Canadian English varieties has been the ongoing focus of systematic studies since the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">Phonologically</a>, Canadian and <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a> are classified together as <a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a>, emphasizing the fact that the vast majority of outsiders, even other native English speakers, cannot distinguish the typical accents of the two countries by sound alone. There are minor disagreements over the degree to which even Canadians and Americans themselves can differentiate their own two accents,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and there is even evidence that some <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western American English</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_English" title="Pacific Northwest English">Pacific Northwest</a> and <a href="/wiki/California_English" title="California English">California English</a>, for example) is undergoing a <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shift" title="Canadian Shift">vowel shift</a> partially coinciding with a vowel shift occurring in mainland Canadian English, first reported in the early 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term "Canadian English" is first attested in a speech by the Reverend A. Constable Geikie in an address to the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Institute">Canadian Institute</a> in 1857 (see <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.dchp.ca/DCHP-1/">DCHP-1 Online</a>, s.v. "Canadian English", Avis <i>et al.,</i> 1967<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>). Geikie, a <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a>-born Canadian, reflected the <a href="/wiki/Anglocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglocentric">Anglocentric</a> attitude that would be prevalent in Canada for the next hundred years when he referred to the language as "a corrupt dialect", in comparison with what he considered the proper English spoken by immigrants from <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dbmtyy_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dbmtyy-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Canadian English is the product of five waves of immigration and settlement over a period of more than two centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The first large wave of permanent English-speaking settlement in Canada, and linguistically the most important, was the influx of <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">Loyalists</a> fleeing the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, chiefly from the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-Atlantic States">Mid-Atlantic States</a>—as such, Canadian English is believed by some scholars to have derived from <a href="/wiki/Northern_American_English" title="Northern American English">northern American English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Canadian English has been developing features of its own since the early 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The second wave from Britain and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> was encouraged to settle in Canada after the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Governors_of_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Governors of Canada">governors of Canada</a>, who were worried about American dominance and influence among its citizens. Further waves of immigration from around the globe peaked in 1910, 1960 and at the present time had a lesser influence, but they did make Canada a <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multicultural</a> country, ready to accept linguistic change from around the world during the current period of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The languages of <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">Aboriginal peoples in Canada</a> started to influence European languages used in Canada even before widespread settlement took place,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_French" title="Quebec French">French</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Canada" title="Lower Canada">Lower Canada</a> provided vocabulary, with words such as <i>toque</i> and <i>portage</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-:1_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> to the English of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">Upper Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dbmtyy_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dbmtyy-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the process of the making of Canadian English—its documentation and codification—goes back to the 1930s,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> the 1960s were the key period.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (November 2019)">verification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Like other social developments in Canada, the general acceptance of Canadian English has taken its time. According to a recent study, a noticeable shift in public discourse can only be seen in the middle of the first decade of the 2000s, when Canadian English was seen as a "given",  generally accepted default variety, while before such statements were usually "balanced" by doubts.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_linguistics">Historical linguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical linguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Studies on earlier forms of English in Canada are rare, yet connections with other work to historical linguistics can be forged. An overview of diachronic work on Canadian English, or diachronically relevant work, is Dollinger (2012, updated to 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Until the 2000s, basically all commentators on the history of CanE have argued from the "language-external" history, i.e. social and political history.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> An exception has been in the area of lexis, where Avis <i>et al.</i>'s 1967 <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Canadianisms_on_Historical_Principles" class="mw-redirect" title="Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles">Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles</a></i> offered real-time historical data through its quotations. Recently, historical linguists have started to study earlier Canadian English with historical linguistic data. DCHP-1 is now available in open access.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Most notably, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/4006034/New-Dialect_Formation_in_Canada_Evidence_from_the_English_Modal_Auxiliaries">Dollinger (2008)</a> pioneered the historical corpus linguistic approach for English in Canada with CONTE (Corpus of Early Ontario English, 1776–1849) and offers a developmental scenario for 18th- and 19th-century Ontario. Recently, Reuter (2015),<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> with a 19th-century newspaper corpus from Ontario, has confirmed the scenario laid out in Dollinger (2008).
</p><p><span id="Canadian_dainty"></span> Historically, Canadian English included a class-based <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">sociolect</a> known as <b>Canadian dainty</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-dainty_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dainty-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Treated as a marker of upper-class prestige in the 19th and early 20th centuries, Canadian dainty was marked by the use of some features of <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> pronunciation, resulting in an accent similar to the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_accent" title="Mid-Atlantic accent">Mid-Atlantic accent</a> known in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-dainty_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dainty-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This accent faded in prominence following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, when it became stigmatized as pretentious, and is now almost never heard in contemporary Canadian life outside of archival recordings used in film, television or radio documentaries.<sup id="cite_ref-dainty_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dainty-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_tendencies">Spelling tendencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spelling tendencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Canadian spelling of the English language combines British and American conventions, the two dominant varieties, and adds some domestic idiosyncrasies. Spelling in Canadian English co-varies with regional and social variables, somewhat more so, perhaps, than in the two dominant varieties of English, yet general trends have emerged since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:International_English_Spelling.png" class="image"><img alt="Canadian spelling in comparison with American and British spelling." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/International_English_Spelling.png/220px-International_English_Spelling.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/International_English_Spelling.png/330px-International_English_Spelling.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/International_English_Spelling.png/440px-International_English_Spelling.png 2x" data-file-width="1078" data-file-height="608" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:International_English_Spelling.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Canadian spelling in comparison with American and British spelling.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Words such as <i>real<b>ize</b></i> and <i>paral<b>yze</b></i> are usually spelled with <i>-ize</i> or <i>-yze</i> rather than <i>-ise</i> or <i>-yse</i>.</li>
<li>French-derived words that in American English end with <i>-or</i> and <i>-er</i>, such as <i>col<b>or</b></i> or <i>cent<b>er</b></i>, often retain British spellings (<i>col<b>our</b></i> and <i>cent<b>re</b></i>).</li>
<li>While the United States uses the Anglo-French spelling <i>defen<b>se</b></i> and <i>offen<b>se</b></i> (noun), most Canadians use the British spellings <i>defen<b>ce</b></i> and <i>offen<b>ce</b></i>. (But <i>defen<b>sive</b></i> and <i>offen<b>sive</b></i> are universal across all forms of English.)</li>
<li>Some nouns, as in British English, take <i>-ice</i> while matching verbs take <i>-ise</i>&#160;– for example, <i>pract<b>ice</b></i> and <i>licen<b>ce</b></i> are nouns while <i>pract<b>ise</b></i> and <i>licen<b>se</b></i> are the respective corresponding verbs. (But <i>adv<b>ice</b></i> and <i>adv<b>ise</b></i> are universal.)</li>
<li>Canadian spelling sometimes retains the British practice of doubling the consonant <i>-l-</i> when adding suffixes to words even when the final syllable (before the suffix) is not stressed. Compare Canadian (and British) <i>trave<b>ll</b>ed</i>, <i>counse<b>ll</b>ing</i>, and <i>marve<b>ll</b>ous</i> (more often than not in Canadian while always doubled in British) to American <i>trave<b>l</b>ed, counse<b>l</b>ing,</i> and <i>marve<b>l</b>ous</i>. In American English, this consonant is only doubled when stressed; thus, for instance, <i>contro<b>ll</b>able</i> and <i>enthra<b>ll</b>ing</i> are universal. (But both Canadian and British English use <i>ballo<b>t</b>ed</i> and <i>profi<b>t</b>ing</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-CanadianOxford_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CanadianOxford-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>)</li>
<li>In other cases, Canadian and American usage differs from British spelling, such as in the case of nouns like <i>curb</i>, <i>tire</i>, and <i>aluminum</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> which in British English are spelled <i>kerb</i>, <i>tyre</i>, and <i>aluminium.</i> <span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">(See below for an explanation of the Canadian spelling of <i>tire</i>.)</span></li></ul>
<p>Canadian spelling conventions can be partly explained by Canada's trade history. For instance, the British spelling of the word <i>cheque</i> probably relates to Canada's once-important ties to British financial institutions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Canada's <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobile</a> industry, on the other hand, has been dominated by American firms from its inception, explaining why Canadians use the American spelling of <i>tire</i> (hence, "<a href="/wiki/Canadian_Tire" title="Canadian Tire">Canadian Tire</a>") and American terminology for automobiles and their parts (for example, <i>truck</i> instead of <i>lorry</i>, <i>gasoline</i> instead of <i>petrol</i>, <i>trunk</i> instead of <i>boot</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CanadianOxford_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CanadianOxford-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Canada's political history has also had an influence on Canadian spelling. Canada's first <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Canada" title="Prime Minister of Canada">prime minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_A._Macdonald" title="John A. Macdonald">John A. Macdonald</a>, once advised the <a href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_Canada" title="Governor General of Canada">Governor General of Canada</a> to issue an <a href="/wiki/Order-in-council" class="mw-redirect" title="Order-in-council">order-in-council</a> directing that government papers be written in the British style.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A contemporary reference for formal Canadian spelling is the spelling used for <a href="/wiki/Hansard" title="Hansard">Hansard</a> transcripts of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Canada" title="Parliament of Canada">Parliament of Canada</a> <span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">(see <i>The Canadian Style</i> in <a href="#Further_reading">Further reading</a> below)</span>. Many Canadian editors, though, use the <i><a href="/wiki/Canadian_Oxford_Dictionary" title="Canadian Oxford Dictionary">Canadian Oxford Dictionary</a></i>, often along with the chapter on spelling in <i>Editing Canadian English</i>, and, where necessary (depending on context), one or more other references. <span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">(See <a href="#Further_reading">Further reading</a> below.)</span>
</p><p>Throughout part of the 20th century, some Canadian newspapers adopted American spellings,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> for example, <i>color</i> as opposed to the British-based <i>colour</i>. Some of the most substantial historical spelling data can be found in Dollinger (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and Grue (2013).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The use of such spellings was the long-standing practice of <a href="/wiki/The_Canadian_Press" title="The Canadian Press">the Canadian Press</a> perhaps since that news agency's inception, but visibly the norm prior to <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OC31Mar1990_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OC31Mar1990-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The practice of dropping the letter <i>u</i> in such words was also considered a labour-saving technique during the early days of printing in which <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a> was set manually.<sup id="cite_ref-OC31Mar1990_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OC31Mar1990-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Canadian newspapers also received much of their international content from American press agencies, therefore it was much easier for editorial staff to leave the spellings from the wire services as provided.<sup id="cite_ref-TS08Mar97_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TS08Mar97-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1990s, Canadian newspapers began to adopt the British spelling variants such as <i>-our</i> endings, notably with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Globe_and_Mail" title="The Globe and Mail">The Globe and Mail</a></i> changing its spelling policy in October 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Other Canadian newspapers adopted similar changes later that decade, such as the <a href="/wiki/Canwest_News_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Canwest News Service">Southam</a> newspaper chain's conversion in September 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Toronto_Star" title="Toronto Star">Toronto Star</a></i> adopted this new spelling policy in September 1997 after that publication's ombudsman discounted the issue earlier in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-TS08Mar97_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TS08Mar97-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TS13Sep97_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TS13Sep97-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Star</i> had always avoided using recognized Canadian spelling, citing the <i><a href="/wiki/Gage_Canadian_Dictionary" title="Gage Canadian Dictionary">Gage Canadian Dictionary</a></i> in their defence. Controversy around this issue was frequent. When the <i>Gage Dictionary</i> finally adopted standard Canadian spelling, the <i>Star</i> followed suit. Some publishers, e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Maclean%27s" title="Maclean&#39;s">Maclean's</a></i>, continue to prefer American spellings.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictionaries">Dictionaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dictionaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Canadian+English%22">"Canadian English"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Canadian+English%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Canadian+English%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Canadian+English%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Canadian+English%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Canadian+English%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The first Canadian dictionaries of Canadian English were edited by Walter Spencer Avis and published by <a href="/wiki/Gage_Educational_Publishing_Company" title="Gage Educational Publishing Company">Gage</a> Ltd. The <i>Beginner's Dictionary</i> (1962), the <i>Intermediate Dictionary</i> (1964) and, finally, the <i>Senior Dictionary</i> (1967) were milestones in Canadian English lexicography. In November 1967 <a href="/wiki/A_Dictionary_of_Canadianisms_on_Historical_Principles" title="A Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles">A Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles</a> (DCHP) was published and completed the first edition of Gage's Dictionary of Canadian English Series. The DCHP documents the historical development of Canadian English words that can be classified as "Canadianisms". It therefore includes words such as mukluk, Canuck, and bluff, but does not list common core words such as desk, table or car. Many secondary schools in Canada use the graded dictionaries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2018)">clarification needed</span></a>&#32;What is a graded dictionary?</i>&#93;</sup> The dictionaries have regularly been updated since: the <i>Senior Dictionary</i> was renamed <i>Gage Canadian Dictionary</i>. Its fifth edition was printed beginning in 1997. Gage was acquired by Thomson Nelson around 2003. The latest editions were published in 2009 by <a href="/wiki/HarperCollins" title="HarperCollins">HarperCollins</a>. On 17 March 2017 a second edition of DCHP, the online <a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Canadianisms_on_Historical_Principles_2" title="Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles 2">Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles 2</a> (DCHP-2), was published. DCHP-2 incorporates the c. 10 000 lexemes from DCHP-1 and adds c. 1 300 novel meanings or 1 002 lexemes to the documented lexicon of Canadian English.
</p><p>In 1997, the <i>ITP Nelson Dictionary of the Canadian English Language</i> was another product, but has not been updated since.
</p><p>In 1998, Oxford University Press produced a <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadian</a> English dictionary, after five years of lexicographical research, entitled <i>The Oxford Canadian Dictionary</i>. A second edition, retitled <i>The Canadian Oxford Dictionary</i>, was published in 2004. Just as the older dictionaries it includes uniquely Canadian words and words borrowed from other languages, and surveyed spellings, such as whether <i>colour</i> or <i>color</i> was the more popular choice in common use. Paperback and concise versions (2005, 2006), with minor updates, are available.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology_and_phonetics">Phonology and phonetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Phonology and phonetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In terms of the major sound systems (<a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonologies</a>) of English around the world, Canadian English aligns most closely to American English, both being grouped together under a common <a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a> sound system; the mainstream Canadian accent ("Standard Canadian") is often compared to the very similar and largely overlapping "General American" accent, an accent widely spoken throughout the United States and perceived there as being relatively lacking in any noticeable regional features.
</p><p>The provinces east of Ontario show the largest dialect diversity. Northern Canada is, according to <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a>, a dialect region in formation, and a homogeneous dialect has not yet formed.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A very homogeneous dialect exists in Western and Central Canada, a situation that is similar to that of the Western United States. Labov identifies an inland region that concentrates all of the defining features of the dialect centred on the Prairies, with periphery areas with more variable patterns including the metropolitan areas of Vancouver and Toronto.<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This dialect forms a dialect continuum with the far <a href="/wiki/Western_American_English" title="Western American English">Western U.S. English</a>, however it is sharply differentiated from the <a href="/wiki/Inland_North" class="mw-redirect" title="Inland North">Inland Northern</a> U.S. English of the central and eastern Great Lakes region.
</p><p>Canadian English raises the diphthong onsets, /ə, ʌ/, before voiceless segments; diphthongs /ai/ and /au/.<sup id="cite_ref-Hickey_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hickey-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Canadian_English">Standard Canadian English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Standard Canadian English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Canadian_English" title="Standard Canadian English">Standard Canadian English</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Standard_Canadian_English" title="Standard Canadian English">Standard Canadian English</a> is socially defined. It is the variety spoken, in Chamber's (1998: 252) definition, by Anglophone or multilingual residents, who are second generation or later (i.e. born in Canada) and who live in urban settings.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Applying this definition, c. 36% of the Canadian population speak Standard Canadian English in the 2006 population, with 38% in the 2011 census.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Canadian Shift encompasses an area from Montreal to Vancouver and can be distinguished by the merger of /ɛ/ and /æ/ before /r/, the fronted /ɑːr/, and a nasal /æ/.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variation">Regional variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Regional variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The literature has for a long time conflated the notions of Standard Canadian English (StCE) and regional variation. While some regional dialects are close with the StCE, they are not identical with it. To the untrained ear, for instance, a B.C. middle class speaker from a rural setting may sound like a StCE speaker, while, given Chambers' definition, such person, because of the rural provenance, would not be included in the accepted definition (see the previous section). The <i>Atlas of North American English,</i> while being the best source for U.S. regional variation, is not a good source for Canadian regional variation, as its analysis is based on only 33<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Canadian speakers. Boberg's (2005, 2008) studies offer the best data for the delimitation of dialect zones. The results for vocabulary<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and phonetics<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> overlap to a great extent, which has allowed the proposal of dialect zones. Dollinger and Clarke<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> distinguish between:
</p>
<ul><li>West (B.C., Alberta, Saskatchewan, Manitoba; with B.C. a sub-zone on the lexical level)</li>
<li>Ontario (with Northwestern Ontario a transition zone with the West)</li>
<li>Quebec (concerning the c. 500 000 Anglophone speakers in the province, not the francophone speakers of English)</li>
<li>Maritimes (P.E.I., N.S., N.B., with P.E.I. a subgroup on the lexical level)</li>
<li>Newfoundland</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Columbia">British Columbia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: British Columbia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_English" title="Pacific Northwest English">Pacific Northwest English</a></div>
<p>British Columbia English shares dialect features with both Standard Canadian English and the American Pacific Northwest English. In <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, speakers exhibit more vowel retraction of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> before nasals than people from <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, and this retraction may become a regional marker of West Coast English.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æɡ/</span> raising (found words such as bag, vague and bagel), a prominent feature in western American speakers, is also found in Vancouver speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">Canadian raising</a> (found in words such as "about" and "writer") is less prominent in B.C. than other parts of the country and is on the decline further, with many speakers not raising <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aɪ/</span> before voiceless consonants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Younger speakers in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Vancouver" title="Greater Vancouver">Greater Vancouver</a> area do not even raise <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/aʊ/</span>, causing "about" to sound somewhat like "a boat". The "o" in such words as <i>holy, goal, load, know,</i> etc. is pronounced as a back and rounded <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[o]</span>, but not as rounded as in the Prairies where there are strong Scandinavian, Slavic and German influences.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontario">Ontario</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ontario">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">Canadian raising</a> is quite strong throughout the province of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, except within the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Valley" title="Ottawa Valley">Ottawa Valley</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shift" title="Canadian Shift">Canadian Shift</a> is also a common <a href="/wiki/Vowel_shift" title="Vowel shift">vowel shift</a> found in Ontario. The retraction of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> was found to be more advanced for women in Ontario than for people from the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Prairies" title="Canadian Prairies">Prairies</a> or <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Canada" title="Atlantic Canada">Atlantic Canada</a> and men.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Ontario" title="Southwestern Ontario">Southwestern Ontario</a> (roughly in the line south from Sarnia to St. Catharines), despite the existence of the many characteristics of West/Central Canadian English, many speakers, especially those under 30, speak a dialect which is influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Inland_Northern_American_English" title="Inland Northern American English">Inland Northern American English</a> dialect, though there are minor differences such as Canadian raising (e.g. "ice" vs "my"). Additionally, there is a tendency to round the mouth after pronouncing the vowel "o" which is distinct from the General American Accent. Also, the vowel of "bag" sounds closer to "vague" or "egg"; "right" sounds like "rate"; and the "ah" vowel in "can't" is drawn out, sounding like "kee-ant".
</p><p>The subregion of <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwestern Ontario">Midwestern Ontario</a> consists of the Counties of <a href="/wiki/Huron_County,_Ontario" title="Huron County, Ontario">Huron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_County,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruce County, Ontario">Bruce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grey_County,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey County, Ontario">Grey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Perth_County,_Ontario" title="Perth County, Ontario">Perth</a>. The "Queen's Bush," as the area was called, did not experience communication with Southwestern and Central dialects until the early 20th century. Thus, a strong accent similar to Central Ontarian is heard, yet many different phrasings exist. It is typical in the area to drop phonetic sounds to make shorter contractions, such as: <i>prolly</i> (probably), <i>goin'</i> (going), and "Wuts goin' on tonight? D'ya wanna do sumthin'?" It is particularly strong in the County of Bruce, so much that it is commonly referred to as being the Bruce Cownian (Bruce Countian) accent. Also 'er' sounds are often pronounced 'air', with "were" sounding more like "wear".
</p><p>Residents of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horseshoe" title="Golden Horseshoe">Golden Horseshoe</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Toronto_Area" title="Greater Toronto Area">Greater Toronto Area</a>) are known to <a href="/wiki/Intervocalic_alveolar_flapping" class="mw-redirect" title="Intervocalic alveolar flapping">merge the second <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> with the <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span></a> in <i>Toronto</i>, pronouncing the name variously as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[toˈɹɒɾ̃o]</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[təˈɹɒɾ̃o]</span> or even <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈtɹɒɾ̃o]</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈtɹɒɾ̃ə]</span>. This, however, is not unique to Toronto; for example, Atlanta is often pronounced "Atlanna" by residents. In the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Toronto_Area" title="Greater Toronto Area">Greater Toronto Area</a>, the <i>th</i> sound <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ð/</span> is often pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[d]</span>. Sometimes <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ð/</span> is elided altogether, resulting in "Do you want this one er'iss one?" The word <i>southern</i> is often pronounced with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[aʊ]</span>. In the area north of the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Municipality_of_York" title="Regional Municipality of York">Regional Municipality of York</a> and south of <a href="/wiki/Parry_Sound,_Ontario" title="Parry Sound, Ontario">Parry Sound</a>, notably among those who were born in the surrounding communities, the cutting down of syllables and consonants often heard, e.g. "probably" is reduced to "prolly" or "probly" when used as a response. In Greater Toronto, the <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a> tends to be fronted (as a result the word <i>about</i> is pronounced as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[əˈbɛʊt]</span> or 'a-beh-oot').
</p><p>The Greater Toronto Area is diverse linguistically, with 44 percent of its people holding a mother tongue other than English.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> As a result Toronto has a distinct variation from other regions.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In Toronto's ethnic communities there are many words that are distinct, particularly those that come from the city's large <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> community. Although only 1.5% of Torontonians speak French, a relatively low proportion of them (56.2%) are native speakers of English, according to the 2006 Census.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> As a result Toronto shows a more variable speech pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Ontario" title="Eastern Ontario">Eastern Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">Canadian raising</a> is not as strong as it is in the rest of the province. In <a href="/wiki/Prescott_and_Russell_United_Counties,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Prescott and Russell United Counties, Ontario">Prescott and Russell</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Stormont,_Dundas_and_Glengarry_United_Counties" class="mw-redirect" title="Stormont, Dundas and Glengarry United Counties">Stormont-Dundas-Glengarry</a> and Eastern <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, French accents are often mixed with English ones due to the high Franco-Ontarian population there. In <a href="/wiki/Lanark_County,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Lanark County, Ontario">Lanark County</a>, Western <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leeds_and_Grenville_United_Counties,_Ontario" class="mw-redirect" title="Leeds and Grenville United Counties, Ontario">Leeds-Grenville</a> and the rest of <a href="/wiki/Stormont,_Dundas_and_Glengarry_United_Counties" class="mw-redirect" title="Stormont, Dundas and Glengarry United Counties">Stormont-Dundas-Glengarry</a>, the accent spoken is nearly identical to that spoken in <a href="/wiki/Central_Ontario" title="Central Ontario">Central Ontario</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quinte" class="mw-redirect" title="Quinte">Quinte</a> area. Phrases such as "got it" is often pronounced as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɡɔɾɪʔ]</span>. <i>Okay</i> is often pronounced as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɔɪke]</span>, while <i>hello</i> is often pronounced as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[helo]</span>.
</p><p>A linguistic enclave has also formed in the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Valley" title="Ottawa Valley">Ottawa Valley</a>, heavily influenced by original <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Irish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> settlers, and existing along the Ontario-Quebec boundary, which has its own distinct accent known as the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Valley_twang" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottawa Valley twang">Ottawa Valley twang</a> (or brogue).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Phonetically, the Ottawa Valley twang is characterized by the lack of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_raising" title="Canadian raising">Canadian raising</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a>, two common elements of mainstream Canadian English. However, this accent is quite rare in the region today.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quebec">Quebec</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Quebec">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quebec_English" title="Quebec English">Quebec English</a></div>
<p>English is a minority language in Quebec (with French the majority), but has many speakers in Montreal, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Townships" title="Eastern Townships">Eastern Townships</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Gatineau" title="Gatineau">Gatineau</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a> region. Uniquely, many people in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> distinguish between words like <i>marry</i> versus <i>merry</i> and <i>parish</i> versus <i>perish</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> which are homophones to most other speakers of Canadian English. A person with English mother tongue and still speaking English as the first language is called an <i>Anglophone</i> versus a French speaker, or <i>Francophone</i>. Quebec Anglophones generally pronounce French street names in Montreal as French words. <i>Pie IX</i> Boulevard is pronounced as in French, not as "pie nine", but as <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ʊ/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;push&#39;">ʊ</span><span title="&#39;f&#39; in &#39;find&#39;">f</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">pee-<span style="font-size:90%">NUUF</span></i></a> (compare French /pi.nœf/). On the other hand, Anglophones do pronounce final <i>d</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> as in <i>Bernard</i> and <i>Bouchard</i>; the word <i>Montreal</i> is pronounced as an English word and <i>Rue Lambert-Closse</i> is known as <i>Clossy Street</i> (vs French /clɔs/).
</p><p>In the city of Montreal, especially in some of the western suburbs like Côte-St-Luc and Hampstead, there is a strong <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jewish</a> influence in the English spoken in these areas. A large wave of Jewish immigration from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> before and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> is also evident today. Their English has a strong <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a> influence; there are some similarities to English spoken in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>. Words used mainly in Quebec and especially in Montreal are:<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <i>stage</i> for "apprenticeship" or "internship", <i>copybook</i> for a notebook, <i>dépanneur</i> or <i>dep</i> for a convenience store, and <i>guichet</i> for an ABM/ATM. It is also common for Anglophones, particularly of Greek or Italian descent, to use translated French words instead of common English equivalents such as "open" and "close" for "on" and "off" or "Open the lights, please" for "Turn on the lights, please".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maritimes">Maritimes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Maritimes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Maritime_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Maritime English">Canadian Maritime English</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG/350px-Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG" decoding="async" width="350" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG/525px-Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG 2x" data-file-width="634" data-file-height="522" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Canada_IPA_chart.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Based on Labov <i>et al.</i>; averaged F1/F2 means for speakers from N.S., N.B., N.L.</div></div></div>
<p>Many in the Maritime provinces&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>&#160;– have an accent that sounds more like <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish English</a> and, in some places, <a href="/wiki/Irish_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish English">Irish English</a> than General American. Outside of major communities, dialects can vary markedly from community to community, as well as from province to province, reflecting ethnic origin as well as a past in which there were few roads and many communities, with some villages very isolated. Into the 1980s, residents of villages in northern Nova Scotia could identify themselves by dialects and accents distinctive to their village. The dialects of Prince Edward Island are often considered the most distinct grouping.
</p><p>The phonology of <a href="/wiki/Maritimer_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritimer English">Maritimer English</a> has some unique features:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Cot–caught</i> merger in effect, but toward a central vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">ä</span>.</li>
<li>No <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shift" title="Canadian Shift">Canadian Shift</a> of the short <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowels</a></li>
<li>Pre-consonantal <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/r/</span> is sometimes (though rarely) deleted.</li>
<li>The flapping of intervocalic <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/d/</span> to alveolar tap <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɾ]</span> between vowels, as well as pronouncing it as a glottal stop <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʔ]</span>, is less common in the Maritimes. Therefore, <i>battery</i> is pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈbætɹi]</span> instead of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ˈbæɾ(ə)ɹi]</span>.</li>
<li>Especially among the older generation, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/w/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/hw/</span> are not merged; that is, the beginning sound of <i>why</i>, <i>white</i>, and <i>which</i> is different from that of <i>witch</i>, <i>with</i>, and <i>wear</i>.</li>
<li>Like most varieties of CanE, Maritimer English contains Canadian raising.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Newfoundland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_English" title="Newfoundland English">Newfoundland English</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> spoken in the province of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a>, an autonomous dominion until 31 March 1949, is often considered the most distinctive Canadian English dialect. Some Newfoundland English differs in <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">pronunciation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>, and preservation of <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">archaic</a> adverbial-intensifiers. The dialect can vary markedly from community to community, as well as from region to region, reflecting ethnic origin as well as a past in which there were few roads and many communities, and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_outport" title="Newfoundland outport">fishing villages</a> in particular remained very isolated. A few speakers have a transitional pin–pen merger.<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aboriginal_north">Aboriginal north</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Aboriginal north">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Aboriginal_syllabics" title="Canadian Aboriginal syllabics">Canadian Aboriginal syllabics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inuit_languages" title="Inuit languages">Inuit languages</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> people from <a href="/wiki/Northern_Canada" title="Northern Canada">Northern Canada</a> speak a version of Canadian English influenced by the phonology of their first languages. European Canadians in these regions are relatively recent arrivals, and have not produced a dialect that is distinct from southern Canadian English.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are a handful of syntactical practices unique to Canadian English. When writing, Canadians may start a sentence with <i>As well</i>, in the sense of "in addition"; this construction is a Canadianism.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike British English, North American English strongly prefers <i>have</i> to <i>have got</i> to denote possession or obligation (as in <i>I have a car</i> vs. <i>I've got a car</i>); Canadian English, however, differs from American English in that it tends to eschew plain <i>got</i> (<i>I got a car</i>), which is a common third option in very informal U.S. English. <sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In speech and in writing, Canadian English speakers permit (and often use) a transitive form for some past participles where only an intransitive form is permitted in most other dialects.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Examples include: "finished something" (rather than "finished with something"), "done something" (rather than "done with something"), "graduated university" (rather than "graduated from university").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_time_notation">Date and time notation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Date and time notation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG/220px-Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG/330px-Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG/440px-Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1756" data-file-height="1174" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queenston_Bridge_Canada_Passport_Bridge.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Canadian_passport" title="Canadian passport">Canadian passport</a> stamp from <a href="/wiki/Queenston_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Queenston Bridge">Queenston Bridge</a>, showing the date 8 June 2014.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Date_and_time_notation_in_Canada" title="Date and time notation in Canada">Date and time notation in Canada</a></div>
<p>Date and time notation in Canadian English is a mixture of British and American practices. The date can be written in the form of either "July 1, 2017" or "1 July 2017": the latter is common in more formal writing and bilingual contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-CollishawDate_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollishawDate-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Canada" title="Government of Canada">Government of Canada</a> only recommends writing all-numeric dates in the form of YYYY-MM-DD (e.g. 2017-07-01), following <a href="/wiki/ISO_8601" title="ISO 8601">ISO 8601</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, the traditional DD/MM/YY and MM/DD/YY systems remain in everyday use, which can be interpreted in multiple ways: 01/07/17 can mean either 1 July 2017 or 7 January 2017. <a href="/wiki/Private_members%27_bills" class="mw-redirect" title="Private members&#39; bills">Private members' bills</a> have repeatedly attempted to clarify the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The government also recommends use of the <a href="/wiki/24-hour_clock" title="24-hour clock">24-hour clock</a>, which is widely used in contexts such as transportation schedules, parking meters, and data transmission.<sup id="cite_ref-CollishawTime_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CollishawTime-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Many users of English use the <a href="/wiki/12-hour_clock" title="12-hour clock">12-hour clock</a> in everyday speech, even when reading from a 24-hour display, similar to the use of the <a href="/wiki/24-hour_clock_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="24-hour clock in the United Kingdom">24-hour clock in the United Kingdom</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Where Canadian English shares vocabulary with other English dialects, it tends to share most with American English, but also has many non-American terms distinctively shared instead with Britain. British and American terms also can coexist in Canadian English to various extents, sometimes with new nuances in meaning; a classic example is <i>holiday</i> (British) often used interchangeably with <i>vacation</i> (American), though, in Canadian speech, the latter can more narrowly mean a trip elsewhere and the former can mean general time off work.  In addition, the vocabulary of Canadian English also features some words that are seldom (if ever) found elsewhere. A good resource for these and other words is the <a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Canadianisms_on_Historical_Principles" class="mw-redirect" title="Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles">Dictionary of Canadianisms on Historical Principles</a>, which is currently being revised at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">University of British Columbia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, British Columbia. The Canadian public appears to take interest in unique "Canadianisms": words that are distinctively characteristic of Canadian English—though perhaps not exclusive to Canada; there is some disagreement about the extent to which "Canadianism" means a term actually unique to Canada, with such an understanding possibly overstated by the popular media.<sup id="cite_ref-Dollinger_2008_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dollinger_2008-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> As a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, Canada shares many items of institutional terminology and professional designations with the countries of the former <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>&#160;– for example, <i>constable</i>, for a police officer of the lowest rank, and <i>chartered accountant</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <i>college</i>, which refers to post-secondary education in general in the U.S., refers in Canada to either a post-secondary technical or vocational institution, or to one of the colleges that exist as <a href="/wiki/Federated_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Federated school">federated schools</a> within some Canadian universities. Most often, a <i>college</i> is a community college, not a university. It may also refer to a <a href="/wiki/CEGEP" title="CEGEP">CEGEP</a> in Quebec. In Canada, <i>college student</i> might denote someone obtaining a diploma in business management (this would be an <a href="/wiki/Associate_degree" title="Associate degree">associate degree</a> in the United States); while <i>university student</i> is the term for someone earning a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor&#39;s degree">bachelor's degree</a>. For that reason, <i>going to college</i> in Canada does not have the same meaning as <i>going to university</i>, unless the speaker or context clarifies the specific level of post-secondary education that is meant.
</p><p>Within the public school system the chief administrator of a school is generally "the principal", as in the United States, but the term is not used preceding his or her name, i.e. "Principal Smith". The assistant to the principal is not titled as "assistant principal", but rather as "vice-principal", although the former is not unknown. This usage is identical to that in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>.
</p><p>Canadian universities publish <i>calendars</i> or <i>schedules</i>, not <i>catalogs</i> as in the U.S.. Canadian students <i>write</i> or <i>take</i> exams (in the U.S., students generally "take" exams while teachers "write" them); they rarely <i>sit</i> them (standard British usage). Those who supervise students during an exam are sometimes called <i>invigilators</i> as in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">Britain</a>, or sometimes <i>proctors</i> as in the U.S; usage may depend on the region or even the individual institution.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Successive years of school are usually referred to as <i>grade one</i>, <i>grade two</i>, and so on. In Quebec, the speaker (if Francophone) will often say <i>primary one</i>, <i>primary two</i> (a direct translation from the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>), and so on; while Anglophones will say <i>grade one</i>, <i>grade two</i>. (Compare American <i>first grade, second grade</i> (sporadically found in Canada), and English/Welsh <i>Year 1, Year 2</i>, Scottish/Nth.Irish <i>Primary 1, Primary 2</i> or <i>P1, P2</i>, and Sth.Irish <i>First Class, Second Class</i> and so on.).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The year of school before grade 1 is usually called "Kindergarten", with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, where it is called "grade primary".
</p><p>In the U.S., the four years of high school are termed the freshman, sophomore, junior, and senior years (terms also used for college years); in Canada, the specific levels are used instead (i.e., "grade nine").<sup id="cite_ref-Speech_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speech-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> As for higher education, only the term <i>freshman</i> (often reduced to <i>frosh</i>) has some currency in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Speech_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speech-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The American usages "sophomore", "junior" and "senior" are not used in Canadian university terminology, or in speech. The specific high-school grades and university years are therefore stated and individualized; for example, <i>the grade 12s failed to graduate</i>; <i>John is in his second year at McMaster</i>. The "first year", "third year" designation also applies to Canadian law school students, as opposed to the common American usage of "1L", "2L" and "3L".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Canadian students use the term <i>marks</i> (more common in England) or <i>grades</i> (more common in the US) to refer to their results; usage is very mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-Speech_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speech-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Units_of_measurement">Units of measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Units of measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike in the United States, use of <a href="/wiki/SI" class="mw-redirect" title="SI">metric</a> units within a majority of (but not all) industries is standard in Canada, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_Canada" title="Metrication in Canada">partial national adoption of the metric system</a> during the mid-to-late 1970s that was eventually stalled; this has spawned some colloquial usages such as <i>klick</i> for kilometre (as also heard in the U.S. military).
</p><p>Nonetheless, <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">US units</a> are still used in many situations. <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">Imperial volumes</a> are also used, albeit very rarely - although many Canadians and Americans mistakenly conflate the measurements systems despite their slight differences from each other.
</p><p>For example, <a href="/wiki/English_Canadians" title="English Canadians">English Canadians</a> state their weight and height in pounds and feet/inches, respectively. This is also the case for many Quebec Francophones. Distances while playing golf are always marked and discussed in yards, though official scorecards may also show metres. Temperatures for cooking are often given in <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">Fahrenheit</a>, while the weather is given in <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius</a>. Directions in the <a href="/wiki/Prairie_provinces" class="mw-redirect" title="Prairie provinces">Prairie provinces</a> are sometimes given using miles, because the country roads generally follow the mile-based grid of the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_Land_Survey" title="Dominion Land Survey">Dominion Land Survey</a>. Motor vehicle speed limits are measured in kilometres per hour.
</p><p>Canadians measure property, both residential and commercial, in square feet exclusively. Fuel efficiency is less frequently discussed in miles per <a href="/wiki/Gallon#US_liquid_gallon" title="Gallon">US gallon</a>, more often the metric L/100&#160;km despite gasoline being sold by the litre. The <a href="/wiki/Letter_(paper_size)" title="Letter (paper size)">Letter</a> <a href="/wiki/Paper_size" title="Paper size">paper size</a> of 8.5&#160;inches × 11&#160;inches is used instead of the international and metric equivalent <a href="/wiki/ISO_216#A_series" title="ISO 216">A4</a> size of 210&#160;mm × 297&#160;mm. Beer cans are 355 mL (12 US oz), while beer bottles are typically 341 mL (12 Imperial oz).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Although Canadian lexicon features both <i>railway</i> and <i>railroad</i>, <i>railway</i> is the usual term in naming (witness <a href="/wiki/Canadian_National_Railway" title="Canadian National Railway">Canadian National Railway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Pacific_Railway" title="Canadian Pacific Railway">Canadian Pacific Railway</a>), though <i>railroad</i> can be heard fairly frequently in some regions; most <a href="/wiki/Rail_terminology" class="mw-redirect" title="Rail terminology">rail terminology</a> in Canada, however, follows American usage (for example, <i>ties</i> and <i>cars</i> rather than <i>sleepers</i> and <i>carriages</i>).</li>
<li>A two-way ticket can be either a <i>round-trip</i> (American term) or a <i>return</i> (British term).</li>
<li>The terms <i>highway</i> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Trans-Canada_Highway" title="Trans-Canada Highway">Trans-Canada Highway</a>), <i>expressway</i> (Central Canada, as in the <a href="/wiki/Gardiner_Expressway" title="Gardiner Expressway">Gardiner Expressway</a>) and <i>freeway</i> (<a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Park_Freeway" title="Sherwood Park Freeway">Sherwood Park Freeway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a>) are often used to describe various high speed roads with varying levels of access control. Generally, but not exclusively, <i>highway</i> refers to any provincially funded road regardless of its access control. Often such roads will be numbered. Similar to the US, the terms <i>expressway</i> and <i>freeway</i> are often used interchangeably to refer to <a href="/wiki/Controlled-access_highways" class="mw-redirect" title="Controlled-access highways">controlled-access highways</a>, that is, divided highways with access only at grade-separated interchanges (for example, a <a href="/wiki/400-series_highways_(Ontario)" class="mw-redirect" title="400-series highways (Ontario)">400-Series Highway</a> in Ontario).</li></ul>
<p>However, <i>expressway</i> may also refer to a <a href="/wiki/Limited-access_road" title="Limited-access road">limited-access road</a> that has control of access but has <a href="/wiki/At-grade" class="mw-redirect" title="At-grade">at-grade</a> junctions, railway crossings (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Harbour_Expressway" title="Harbour Expressway">Harbour Expressway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thunder_Bay" title="Thunder Bay">Thunder Bay</a>.) Sometimes the term <i><a href="/wiki/Parkway_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Parkway (disambiguation)">Parkway</a></i> is also used (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Hanlon_Parkway" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanlon Parkway">Hanlon Parkway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guelph" title="Guelph">Guelph</a>). In <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, the term 'grid road' is used to refer to minor highways or rural roads, usually gravel, referring to the 'grid' upon which they were originally designed. In <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, freeways and expressways are called <a href="/wiki/Autoroutes_of_Quebec" title="Autoroutes of Quebec">autoroutes</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, the generic <i>Trail</i> is often used to describe a freeway, expressway or major urban street (for example, <a href="/wiki/Deerfoot_Trail" title="Deerfoot Trail">Deerfoot Trail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macleod_Trail" title="Macleod Trail">Macleod Trail</a> or <a href="/wiki/Crowchild_Trail" title="Crowchild Trail">Crowchild Trail</a> in <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellowhead_Trail" title="Yellowhead Trail">Yellowhead Trail</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a>). The British term <i><a href="/wiki/Motorway" class="mw-redirect" title="Motorway">motorway</a></i> is not used. The American terms <i><a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">turnpike</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tollway" class="mw-redirect" title="Tollway">tollway</a></i> for a toll road are not common. The term <i>throughway</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Freeway" class="mw-redirect" title="Freeway">thruway</a></i> was used for first tolled limited-access highways (for example, the Deas Island Throughway, now Highway 99, from <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, BC, to <a href="/wiki/Blaine,_Washington" title="Blaine, Washington">Blaine, Washington</a>, USA or the Saint John Throughway (Highway 1) in <a href="/wiki/Saint_John,_NB" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint John, NB">Saint John, NB</a>), but this term is not common anymore. In everyday speech, when a particular roadway is not being specified, the term <i>highway</i> is generally or exclusively used.
</p>
