<ul><li>A railway at-grade junction can be called a <i><a href="/wiki/Level_crossing" title="Level crossing">level crossing</a>,</i> as well as the term <i><a href="/wiki/Grade_crossing" class="mw-redirect" title="Grade crossing">grade crossing</a></i>, which is commonly used in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>A railway or highway crossing overhead is an <i>overpass</i> or <i>underpass</i>, depending on which part of the crossing is referred to (the two are used more or less interchangeably); the British term <i>flyover</i> is sometimes used in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, and in the Maritimes as well as on occasion in the prairies (such as the 4th avenue flyover in Calgary, Alberta), <i>subway</i> is also used.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>In Quebec, English speakers often use the word "metro" to mean <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">subway</a>. Non-native Anglophones of Quebec will also use the designated proper title "metro" to describe the Montreal subway system.</li>
<li>The term <i>Texas gate</i> refers to the type of <a href="/wiki/Cattle_grid" title="Cattle grid">metal grid</a> called a <i>cattle guard</i> in American English or a <i>cattle grid</i> in British English.</li>
<li>Depending on the region, large trucks used to transport and deliver goods are referred to as 'transport trucks' (E.g. used in Ontario and Alberta) or 'transfer trucks' (E.g. used in Prince Edward Island)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>While in standard usage the terms <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">prime minister</a> and <a href="/wiki/Premier" title="Premier">premier</a> are interchangeable terms for the head of an elected parliamentary government, Canadian English today generally follows a usage convention of reserving the title <i>prime minister</i> for the federal first minister and referring to provincial or territorial leaders as <i>premiers</i>. However, because <a href="/wiki/Canadian_French" title="Canadian French">Canadian French</a> does not have separate terms for the two positions, using <i lang="fr" title="French language text">premier ministre</i> for both, the title <i>prime minister</i> is sometimes seen in reference to a provincial leader when a francophone is speaking or writing English. Also, until the 1970s the leader of the Ontario provincial government was officially styled <i>prime minister</i>.</li>
<li>When a majority of the elected members of the House of Commons or a provincial legislature are not members of the same party as the government, the situation is referred to as a minority government rather than a hung Parliament.</li>
<li>To <i>table</i> a document in Canada is to introduce or present it (as in Britain), whereas in the U.S. it means to postpone consideration until a later date, often indefinitely.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>In Canada, a committee is <i>struck</i>, whereas in the U.S. committees are appointed, formed, or created, etc.</li>
<li>Several political terms are more in use in Canada than elsewhere, including <i>riding</i> (as a general term for a <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Canada" title="Parliament of Canada">parliamentary</a> constituency or <a href="/wiki/Electoral_district_(Canada)" title="Electoral district (Canada)">electoral district</a>). The term <i><a href="/wiki/Reeve_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reeve (Canada)">reeve</a></i> was at one time common for the equivalent of a mayor in some smaller municipalities in <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, but is now falling into disuse. The title is still used for the leader of a rural municipality in <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a>.</li>
<li>The term <i>Tory,</i> used in Britain with a <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">similar meaning</a>, denotes a supporter of the present-day federal <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservative Party of Canada</a>, the historic <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Progressive Conservative Party of Canada">federal</a> or provincial Progressive Conservative Party. The term <a href="/wiki/Red_Tory" title="Red Tory">Red Tory</a> is also used to denote the more socially liberal wings of the Tory parties. <a href="/wiki/Blue_Tory" title="Blue Tory">Blue Tory</a> is less commonly used, and refers to more strict fiscal (rather than social) conservatism. The U.S. use of <i>Tory</i> to mean the Loyalists in the time of the American Revolution is not used in Canada,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> where they are called <a href="/wiki/United_Empire_Loyalists" class="mw-redirect" title="United Empire Loyalists">United Empire Loyalists</a>, or simply Loyalists.</li>
<li>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberal Party of Canada</a> or a provincial Liberal party are sometimes referred to as <i>Grits</i>. Historically, the term comes from the phrase <i>Clear Grit</i>, used in <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian times</a> in Canada to denote an object of quality or a truthful person. The term was assumed as a nickname by Liberals by the 1850s.</li>
<li>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Bloc_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Bloc Québécois">Bloc Québécois</a> are sometimes referred to as <i>Bloquistes</i>. At the purely provincial level, members of Quebec's <a href="/wiki/Parti_Qu%C3%A9b%C3%A9cois" title="Parti Québécois">Parti Québécois</a> are often referred to as <i>Péquistes</i>, and members of the Quebec provincial <a href="/wiki/Action_d%C3%A9mocratique_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Action démocratique du Québec">Action démocratique du Québec</a> as <i>Adéquistes</i>.</li>
<li>The term "Socred" is no longer common due to its namesake party's decline, but referred to members of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_social_credit_movement" title="Canadian social credit movement">Social Credit Party</a>, and was particularly common in British Columbia. It was not used for Social Credit members from Quebec, nor generally used for the federal caucus of that party; in both cases <i>Créditiste</i>, the French term, was used in English.</li>
<li>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Canada" title="Senate of Canada">Senate</a> are referred to by the title "Senator" preceding their name, as in the United States. Members of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_Canada" title="House of Commons of Canada">House of Commons of Canada</a>, following British parliamentary nomenclature, are termed "Members of Parliament", and are referred to as "Jennifer Jones, MP" during their term of office only. Senators, and members of the Privy Council are styled "The Honourable" for life, and the Prime Minister of Canada is styled "The Right Honourable" for life, as is the Chief Justice of the Supreme Court and the Governor General. This honorific may also be bestowed by Parliament, as it was to retiring deputy prime minister <a href="/wiki/Herb_Gray" title="Herb Gray">Herb Gray</a> in 1996. Members of provincial legislatures do not have a pre-nominal style, except in certain provinces, such as Nova Scotia where members of the Queen's <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Nova_Scotia" title="Executive Council of Nova Scotia">Executive Council of Nova Scotia</a> are styled "The Honourable" for life, and are entitled to the use of the post-nominal letters "ECNS".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Cabinet of Ontario serves concurrently (and not for life) as the <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Ontario" title="Executive Council of Ontario">Executive Council of Ontario</a>, while serving members are styled "The Honourable", but are not entitled to post-nominal letters.</li>
<li>Members of provincial/territorial legislative assemblies are called <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Legislative_Assembly" title="Member of the Legislative Assembly">MLAs</a> in all provinces and territories except: <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, where they have been called <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Provincial_Parliament_(Ontario)" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Provincial Parliament (Ontario)">Members of Provincial Parliament</a> (MPPs) since 1938; <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, where they have been called <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_National_Assembly_(Quebec)" title="Member of the National Assembly (Quebec)">Members of the National Assembly</a> (MNAs) since 1968; and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a>, where they are called <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador_House_of_Assembly" title="Newfoundland and Labrador House of Assembly">Members of the House of Assembly</a> (MHAs).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lawyer" title="Lawyer">Lawyers</a> in all parts of Canada, except <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, which has its own <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> system, are called "<a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barristers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solicitor" title="Solicitor">solicitors</a>" because any lawyer licensed in any of the common law provinces and territories must pass bar exams for, and is permitted to engage in, both types of legal practice in contrast to other common-law jurisdictions such as <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> where the two are traditionally separated (i.e., Canada has a <a href="/wiki/Fused_profession" title="Fused profession">fused legal profession</a>). The words <i>lawyer</i> and <i>counsel</i> (not <i>counsellor</i>) predominate in everyday contexts; the word <i>attorney</i> refers to any personal representative. Canadian lawyers generally do not refer to themselves as "attorneys", a term that is common in the United States.
</p><p>The equivalent of an American <i><a href="/wiki/District_attorney" title="District attorney">district attorney</a></i>, meaning the barrister representing the state in criminal proceedings, is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Crown_attorney" title="Crown attorney">crown attorney</a></i> (in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>), <i>crown counsel</i> (in British Columbia), <i>crown prosecutor</i> or <i>the crown</i>, on account of Canada's status as a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> in which <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a> is the locus of state power.
</p><p>The words <i>advocate</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Civil_law_notary" title="Civil law notary">notary</a></i>&#160;– two distinct professions in Quebec civil law&#160;– are used to refer to that province's approximate equivalents of barrister and solicitor, respectively. It is not uncommon, however, for English-speaking advocates in Quebec to refer to themselves in English as "barrister(s) and solicitor(s)", as most advocates chiefly perform what would traditionally be known as "solicitor's work", while only a minority of advocates actually appear in court. In Canada's <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> provinces and territories, the word <i>notary</i> means strictly a <a href="/wiki/Notary_public" title="Notary public">notary public</a>.
</p><p>Within the Canadian legal community itself, the word <i><a href="/wiki/Solicitor" title="Solicitor">solicitor</a></i> is often used to refer to any Canadian lawyer in general (much like the way the word <i>attorney</i> is used in the United States to refer to any American lawyer in general). Despite the conceptual distinction between <i>barrister</i> and <i>solicitor</i>, Canadian court documents would contain a phrase such as "<i>John Smith, </i>solicitor<i> for the Plaintiff</i>" even though "John Smith" may well himself be the barrister who argues the case in court. In a letter introducing him/herself to an opposing lawyer, a Canadian lawyer normally writes something like "<i>I am the </i>solicitor" for Mr. Tom Jones."
</p><p>The word <i>litigator</i> is also used by lawyers to refer to a fellow lawyer who specializes in lawsuits even though the more traditional word <i>barrister</i> is still employed to denote the same specialization.
</p><p>Judges of Canada's superior courts, which exist at the provincial and territorial levels, are traditionally addressed as <i>"My Lord"</i> or <i>"My Lady"</i>, however there are some variances across certain jurisdictions, with some superior court judges preferring the titles <i>"Mister Justice"</i> or <i>"Madam Justice"</i> to <i>"Lordship"</i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Master_(judiciary)" title="Master (judiciary)">Masters</a> are addressed as <i>"Mr. Master"</i> or simply <i>"Sir."</i> In British Columbia, masters are addressed as <i>"Your Honour."</i>
</p><p>Judges of provincial or inferior courts are traditionally referred to in person as <i>"Your Honour"</i>. Judges of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Canada" title="Supreme Court of Canada">Supreme Court of Canada</a> and of the federal-level courts prefer the use of <i>"Mister/Madam (Chief) Justice"</i>. <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_The_Peace#Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice of The Peace">Justices of The Peace</a> are addressed as <i>"Your Worship"</i>. <i>"Your Honour"</i> is also the correct form of address for a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant-Governor_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lieutenant-Governor (Canada)">Lieutenant Governor</a>.
</p><p>A serious crime is called an <a href="/wiki/Indictable_offence" title="Indictable offence">indictable offence</a>, while a less-serious crime is called a <a href="/wiki/Summary_offence" title="Summary offence">summary offence</a>. The older words <a href="/wiki/Felony" title="Felony">felony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Misdemeanour" class="mw-redirect" title="Misdemeanour">misdemeanour</a>, which are still used in the United States, are not used in Canada's current <i><a href="/wiki/Criminal_Code_(Canada)" title="Criminal Code (Canada)">Criminal Code</a></i> (R.S.C. 1985, c. C-46) or by today's Canadian legal system. As noted throughout the <i>Criminal Code</i>, a person accused of a crime is called <i>the accused</i> and not <i>the defendant</i>, a term used instead in civil lawsuits.
</p><p>In Canada, <i><a href="/wiki/Visible_minority" title="Visible minority">visible minority</a></i> refers to a non-aboriginal person or group visibly not one of the majority race in a given population. The term comes from the <a href="/wiki/Employment_equity_(Canada)" title="Employment equity (Canada)">Canadian Employment Equity Act</a>, which defines such people as "persons, other than <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_of_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal peoples of Canada">Aboriginal people</a>, who are non-<a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">Caucasian</a> in race or non-<a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a> in <a href="/wiki/Skin_colour" class="mw-redirect" title="Skin colour">colour</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The term is used as a demographic category by <a href="/wiki/Statistics_Canada" title="Statistics Canada">Statistics Canada</a>. The qualifier "visible" is used to distinguish such minorities from the "invisible" minorities determined by <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Canada" title="Languages of Canada">language</a> (English vs. <a href="/wiki/Canadian_French" title="Canadian French">French</a>) and certain distinctions in <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Canada" title="Religion in Canada">religion</a> (<a href="/wiki/Catholicism_in_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism in Canada">Catholics</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Orange_Order_in_Canada" title="Orange Order in Canada">Protestants</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_British_Columbia" title="Counties of British Columbia">county in British Columbia</a> means only a regional jurisdiction of the courts and justice system and is not otherwise connected to governance as with counties in other provinces and in the United States. The rough equivalent to "county" as used elsewhere is a "<a href="/wiki/Regional_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional District">Regional District</a>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Places">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Distinctive Canadianisms are:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Bachelor" title="Bachelor">bachelor</a></i>: bachelor apartment, an apartment all in a single room, with a small bathroom attached ("They have a bachelor for rent").<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The usual American term is <i>studio</i>. In Quebec, this is known as a <i>one-and-a-half</i> apartment; some Canadians, especially in Prince Edward Island, call it a <i>loft</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>camp</i>: in Northern Ontario, it refers to what is called a <i>cottage</i> in the rest of Ontario and a <i>cabin</i> in the West.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> It is also used, to a lesser extent, in New Brunswick, Nova Scotia, as well as in parts of New England. It generally refers to vacation houses in rural areas.</li>
<li><i>fire hall</i>: <a href="/wiki/Fire_station" title="Fire station">fire station</a>, firehouse.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>height of land</i>: a <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">drainage divide</a>. Originally American.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>parkade</i>: a <a href="/wiki/Parking_garage" class="mw-redirect" title="Parking garage">parking garage</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada" title="Western Canada">the West</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Washroom" class="mw-redirect" title="Washroom">washroom</a></i>:<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> the general term for what is normally named <i>public toilet</i> or <i>lavatory</i> in Britain. In the United States (where it originated) the word was mostly replaced by <i>restroom</i> in the 20th century. Generally used only as a technical or commercial term outside of Canada. The word <i>bathroom</i> is also used.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Indian_reserve" title="Indian reserve">Indian reserve</a></i>, rather than the U.S. term <i>federal Indian reservation</i>. Often shortened to <i>reserve</i>, especially when the meaning is clear from context; another slang variant of this term is the shortened <i>res</i> or (more commonly) <i>rez</i>. Not to be confused with <i>res</i>, which in the context of universities refers strictly to <i>residences</i> or <i>halls of residence</i> (compare to the US American <i>dorms</i> or <i>dormitories</i>). Therefore, the sentences <i>when I lived on rez</i> and <i>when I lived in res</i> mean very different things.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rancherie" title="Rancherie">rancherie</a></i>: the residential area of a First Nation reserve, used in BC only.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Quiggly_hole" title="Quiggly hole">quiggly hole</a></i> and/or <i>quiggly</i>: the depression in the ground left by a <i><a href="/wiki/Kekuli" class="mw-redirect" title="Kekuli">kekuli</a></i> or pithouse. Groups of them are called "quiggly hole towns". Used in the BC Interior only.</li>
<li><i>gas bar</i>: a <a href="/wiki/Filling_station" title="Filling station">filling station</a> (gas station) with a central island, having pumps under a fixed metal or concrete awning.</li>
<li><i>booze can</i>: an after-hours establishment where alcohol is served, often illegally.</li>
<li><i>dépanneur</i>, or the diminutive form <i>dep</i>, is often used by English speakers in Quebec. This is because <a href="/wiki/Convenience_stores" class="mw-redirect" title="Convenience stores">convenience stores</a> are called <i>dépanneurs</i> in Canadian French.</li>
<li><i>snye</i>, a side-stream channel that rejoins a larger river, creating an island.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Daily_life">Daily life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Daily life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Terms common in Canada, Britain and Ireland but less frequent or nonexistent in the United States are:
</p>
<ul><li><i>tin</i> (as in <i>tin of tuna</i>), for <i>can</i>, especially among older speakers. Among younger speakers, <i>can</i> is more common, with <i>tin</i> referring to a can which is wider than it is tall as in "a tin of sardines" as opposed to a "can of soup".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li><i>cutlery</i>, for <i>silverware</i> or <i>flatware</i>, where the material of which the utensil is made is not of consequence to the context in which it is used.</li>
<li><i>serviette</i>, especially in Eastern Canada, for a paper table <i>napkin</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tap_(valve)" title="Tap (valve)">tap</a></i>, conspicuously more common than <i>faucet</i> in everyday usage.</li></ul>
<p>The following are more or less distinctively Canadian:
</p>
<ul><li><i>ABM</i>, <i>bank machine</i>: synonymous with <a href="/wiki/Automated_teller_machine" title="Automated teller machine">ATM</a> (which is also used, but much more widely than ABM by financial organizations in the country).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>BFI bin</i>: Dumpster, after a prominent Canadian waste management company, BFI Canada (which was eventually bought out and merged to became <a href="/wiki/Waste_Connections_of_Canada" title="Waste Connections of Canada">Waste Connections of Canada</a>) in provinces where that company does business; compare to other <a href="/wiki/Generic_trademark" title="Generic trademark">generic trademarks</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Kleenex" title="Kleenex">Kleenex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xerox" title="Xerox">Xerox</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Dumpster" title="Dumpster">Dumpster</a> itself.</li>
<li><i>chesterfield</i>: originally British and internationally used (as in classic furnishing terminology) to refer to a sofa whose arms are the same height as the back, it is a term for <i>any</i> couch or sofa in Canada (and, to some extent, Northern California).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Once a hallmark of CanE, <i>chesterfield</i>, as with <i>settee</i> and <i>davenport</i>, is now largely in decline among younger generations in the western and central regions.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <i>Couch</i> is now the most common term; <i>sofa</i> is also used.</li>
<li><i>dart</i>: cigarette, used primarily by adolescents and young adults.</li>
<li><i>dressing gown</i> or <i>housecoat</i>: in the United States, called a <i>bathrobe</i>.</li>
<li><i>eavestrough</i>: <a href="/wiki/Rain_gutter" title="Rain gutter">rain gutter</a>. Also used, especially in the past, in the Northern and Western United States; the first recorded usage is in <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Moby-Dick" title="Moby-Dick">Moby-Dick</a></i>: "The tails tapering down that way, serve to carry off the water, d'ye see. Same with cocked hats; the cocks form gable-end eave-troughs &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93;, Flask."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>flush</i>: toilet, used primarily by older speakers throughout the Maritimes.</li>
<li><i>garburator</i>: (rhymes with <i>carburetor</i>) a <a href="/wiki/Garbage_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Garbage disposal">garbage disposal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>homogenized milk</i> or <i>homo milk</i>: milk containing 3.25% milk fat, typically called "whole milk" in the United States.</li>
<li><i>hydro</i>: a common synonym for <a href="/wiki/Mains_electricity" title="Mains electricity">electrical service</a>, used primarily in New Brunswick, Quebec, Ontario, Manitoba and British Columbia. Most of the power in these provinces is <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, and suppliers' company names incorporate the term "Hydro". Usage: "I didn't pay my hydro bill so they shut off my lights." Hence <i>hydrofield</i> or <i>hydro corridor</i>, a line of electricity transmission towers, usually in groups cutting across a city, and <i>hydro lines/poles</i>, electrical transmission lines/poles.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> These usages of <i>hydro</i> are also standard in the Australian state of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>. Also in slang usage can refer to hydroponically grown marijuana.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Loonie" title="Loonie">loonie</a></i>: the Canadian one-dollar coin; derived from the use of the <a href="/wiki/Common_loon" title="Common loon">common loon</a> on the reverse. The <i><a href="/wiki/Toonie" title="Toonie">toonie</a></i> (less commonly spelled <i>tooney</i>, <i>twooney</i>, <i>twoonie</i>) is the two-dollar coin. <i>Loonie</i> is also used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">Canadian currency</a>, particularly when discussing the exchange rate with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">U.S. dollar</a>; <i>loonie</i> and <i>toonie</i> describe coinage specifically. (for example, "I have a dollar in pennies" versus "I have three loonies in my pocket").</li>
<li><i>pencil crayon</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-:0_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> coloured pencil.</li>
<li><i>pogie</i> or <i>pogey</i>: term referring to unemployment insurance, which is now officially called <i>Employment</i> Insurance in Canada. Derived from the use of <i>pogey</i> as a term for a poorhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Not used for welfare, in which case the term is <i>"the dole"</i>, as in <i>"he's on the dole, eh?"</i>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Multistorey_car_park" title="Multistorey car park">parkade</a>:</i> multistorey parking garage.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apparel">Apparel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Apparel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The following are common in Canada, but not in the United States or the United Kingdom.
</p>
<ul><li><i>runners</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> running shoes, especially in <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada" title="Western Canada">Western Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Also used in <a href="/wiki/Australian_English" title="Australian English">Australian English</a><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish English">Irish English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Atlantic Canada prefers <i>sneakers</i> while central Canada (including Quebec and Ontario) prefers <i>running shoes</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Touque" class="mw-redirect" title="Touque">touque</a></i> (also spelled <i>toque</i> or <i>tuque</i>): a knitted winter hat. A similar hat would be called a <i>beanie</i> in the western United States and a <i>watch cap</i> in the eastern United States, though these forms are generally closer-fitting, and may lack a brim as well as a pompom. There seems to be no exact equivalent outside Canada, since the tuque is of French Canadian origin.</li>
<li><i>bunnyhug</i>: a hooded sweatshirt, with or without a zipper. Used mainly in Saskatchewan.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_and_beverage">Food and beverage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Food and beverage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>Most Canadians as well as Americans in the Northwest, North Central, Prairie and Inland North prefer <i>pop</i> over <i>soda</i> to refer to a carbonated beverage (though neither term is dominant in British English). <i>Soft drink</i> is also extremely common throughout Canada.</li>
<li>What Americans call <i>Canadian bacon</i> is named <i><a href="/wiki/Back_bacon" title="Back bacon">back bacon</a></i> in Canada, or, if it is coated in cornmeal or ground peas, <i>cornmeal bacon</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Peameal_bacon" title="Peameal bacon">peameal bacon</a></i>.</li>
<li>What most Americans call a <i><a href="/wiki/Chocolate_bar" title="Chocolate bar">candy bar</a></i> is usually known as a <i>chocolate bar</i> (as in the United Kingdom). In certain areas surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a>, it is sometimes known as a <i>nut bar</i>; however, this use is more popular amongst older generations. Legally only bars made of solid chocolate may be labelled chocolate bars, others must be labelled as candy bars.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Even though the terms <i>French fries</i> and <i>fries</i> are used by Canadians, some speakers use the word <i>chips</i> (and its diminutive, <i>chippies</i>) (chips is always used when referring to <i><a href="/wiki/Fish_and_chips" title="Fish and chips">fish and chips</a></i>, as elsewhere).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whole-wheat_bread" class="mw-redirect" title="Whole-wheat bread">whole-wheat bread</a> is often referred to as <i>brown bread</i>, as in "Would you like white or brown bread for your toast?"</li>
<li>An <i><a href="/wiki/Expiry_date" class="mw-redirect" title="Expiry date">expiry date</a></i> is the term used for the date when a perishable product will go bad (similar to the UK <i>Use By</i> date). The term <i>expiration date</i> is more common in the United States (where <i>expiry date</i> is seen mostly on the packaging of Asian food products). The term <i>Best Before</i> also sees common use, where although not spoiled, the product may not taste "as good".</li>
<li><i>double-double</i>: a cup of coffee with two measures of cream and two of sugar,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> most commonly associated with the <a href="/wiki/Tim_Hortons" title="Tim Hortons">Tim Hortons</a> chain of coffee shops.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Canadianisms relating to alcohol:
<ul><li><i>mickey</i>: a 375&#160;mL (12.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/US_fluid_ounce" class="mw-redirect" title="US fluid ounce">US&#160;fl&#160;oz</a>; 13.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Imperial_fluid_ounce" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial fluid ounce">imp&#160;fl&#160;oz</a>) bottle of hard liquor (informally called a <a href="/wiki/Pint" title="Pint">pint</a> in the Maritimes and the United States). In Newfoundland, this is almost exclusively referred to as a "<a href="/wiki/Hip_flask" title="Hip flask">flask</a>". In the United States, "mickey", or "<a href="/wiki/Mickey_Finn_(drugs)" title="Mickey Finn (drugs)">Mickey Finn</a>", refers to a drink laced with drugs.</li>
<li><i>two-six</i>, <i>twenty-sixer</i>, <i>twixer</i>: a 750&#160;mL (25&#160;US&#160;fl&#160;oz; 26&#160;imp&#160;fl&#160;oz) bottle of hard liquor (called a quart in the Maritimes). The word <i>handle</i> is less common. Similarly, a 1.14&#160;L (39&#160;US&#160;fl&#160;oz; 40&#160;imp&#160;fl&#160;oz) bottle of hard liquor is known as a <i>forty</i> and a 1.75&#160;L (59&#160;US&#160;fl&#160;oz; 62&#160;imp&#160;fl&#160;oz) bottle is known as a <i>sixty</i> or <i>half gallon</i> in Nova Scotia.</li>
<li><i>Texas mickey</i> (especially in Saskatchewan, New Brunswick and Nova Scotia; more often a "Saskatchewan mickey" in western Canada): a 3&#160;L (101&#160;US&#160;fl&#160;oz; 106&#160;imp&#160;fl&#160;oz) bottle of hard liquor. (Despite the name, Texas mickeys are generally unavailable outside of Canada.)</li>
<li><i>two-four</i>: a case of 24 beers, also known as a <i>case</i> in Eastern Canada, or a <i>flat</i> in Western Canada (referencing that cans of beer are often sold in packages of six, with four packages to a flat box for shipping and stacking purposes).</li>
<li><i>six-pack</i>, <i>half-sack</i>, <i>half-case</i>, or <i>poverty-pack</i>: a case of six beers</li></ul></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Poutine" title="Poutine">poutine</a></i>: a snack of french fries topped with cheese curds and hot gravy.</li>
<li>There are also <a href="/wiki/Genericized_trademark" class="mw-redirect" title="Genericized trademark">genericized trademarks</a> used in Canada:
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Cheezies" title="Cheezies">cheezies</a></i>: <a href="/wiki/Cheese_puffs" title="Cheese puffs">cheese puffs</a>. The name is a <a href="/wiki/Genericized_trademark" class="mw-redirect" title="Genericized trademark">genericized trademark</a> based on a brand of crunchy cheese snack sold in Canada.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kraft_Dinner" title="Kraft Dinner">Kraft Dinner</a> or "KD": for any packaged dry macaroni and cheese mix, even when it is not produced by Kraft.</li></ul></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Freezie" title="Freezie">freezie</a></i>: A frozen flavoured sugar water snack common worldwide, but known by this name exclusively in Canada.</li>
<li><i>dainty</i>: a fancy cookie, pastry, or square served at a social event (usually plural). Used in western Canada.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Smarties" title="Smarties">Smarties</a></i>: a bean-sized, small candy-covered chocolate, similar to plain <a href="/wiki/M%26M%27s" title="M&amp;M&#39;s">M&amp;M's</a>. This is also seen in British English. <a href="/wiki/Smarties_(tablet_candy)" title="Smarties (tablet candy)">Smarties in the United States</a> refer to small tart powdered disc sold in rolls; in Canada these tart candies are sold as "<a href="/wiki/Rockets_(wafer_candy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rockets (wafer candy)">Rockets</a>".</li></ul>
<h3><span id="Prairies_.28Manitoba.2C_Saskatchewan_and_Alberta.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prairies_(Manitoba,_Saskatchewan_and_Alberta)">Prairies (Manitoba, Saskatchewan and Alberta)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Prairies (Manitoba, Saskatchewan and Alberta)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A strong Canadian raising exists in the prairie regions together with certain older usages such as <i>chesterfield</i> and <i>front room</i> also associated with the Maritimes. Aboriginal Canadians are a larger and more conspicuous population in prairie cities than elsewhere in the country and certain elements of aboriginal speech in English are sometimes to be heard. Similarly, the linguistic legacy, mostly intonation but also speech patterns and syntax, of the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slav" class="mw-redirect" title="Slav">Slavic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> settlers&#160;– who are far more numerous and historically important in the Prairies than in Ontario or the Maritimes&#160;– can be heard in the general milieu. Again, the large <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="Métis people (Canada)">Métis</a> population in <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> also carries with it certain linguistic traits inherited from <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a>, Aboriginal and <a href="/wiki/Celt" class="mw-redirect" title="Celt">Celtic</a> forebears.
Some terms are derived from immigrant groups or are just local inventions:
</p>
<ul><li>bluff: small group of trees isolated by <a href="/wiki/Prairie" title="Prairie">prairie</a></li>
<li>bunny hug: elsewhere <i><a href="/wiki/Hoodie" title="Hoodie">hoodie</a></i> or <i>hooded sweat shirt</i> (mainly in Saskatchewan, but also in Manitoba)</li>
<li>ginch/gonch/gitch/gotch: underwear (usually men's or boys' underwear, more specifically briefs; whereas women's underwear are <i>gotchies</i>), probably of Eastern European or Ukrainian origin. <i>Gitch</i> and <i>gotch</i> are primarily used in Saskatchewan and Manitoba while the variants with an <i>n</i> are common in Alberta and British Columbia.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>jam buster: jelly-filled doughnut.</li>
<li>porch climber: <a href="/wiki/Moonshine" title="Moonshine">moonshine</a> or homemade alcohol. <i>Porch climber</i> has a slightly distinguished meaning in Ontario where it refers to a beverage mixed of beer, vodka, and lemonade.</li>
<li>slough: pond&#160;– usually a pond on a farm</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vi-Co" title="Vi-Co">Vi-Co</a>: occasionally used in Saskatchewan instead of <i>chocolate milk</i>. Formerly a brand of chocolate milk.</li></ul>
<p>In farming communities with substantial <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukrainian</a>, German or <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mennonite">Mennonite</a> populations, accents, sentence structure and vocabulary influenced by these languages is common. These communities are most common in the <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan_Valley" title="Saskatchewan Valley">Saskatchewan Valley</a> region of Saskatchewan and <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Valley" title="Red River Valley">Red River Valley</a> region of Manitoba.
</p><p>Descendants of marriages between Hudson's Bay Company workers of mainly Scottish descent and Cree women spoke <a href="/wiki/Bungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bungi">Bungi</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole</a> that blends Cree and English. A few Bungi speakers can still be found in Manitoba. It is marked by no masculine, feminine or third-person pronouns.<sup id="cite_ref-Hickey_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hickey-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Columbia_2">British Columbia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: British Columbia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>British Columbian English has several words still in current use borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Chinook_Jargon" title="Chinook Jargon">Chinook Jargon</a> although the use of such vocabulary is observably decreasing. The most famous and widely used of these terms are <i><a href="/wiki/Skookum" title="Skookum">skookum</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chinook_Jargon_use_by_English-language_speakers" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinook Jargon use by English-language speakers">saltchuck</a></i>. However, among young <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbians</a>, almost no one uses this vocabulary, and only a small percentage is even familiar with the meaning of such words.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>, <i>cheechako</i> is used for newcomers or greenhorns.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ontario">Northern Ontario</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Northern Ontario">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ontario" title="Northern Ontario">Northern Ontario</a> English has several distinct qualities stemming from its large <a href="/wiki/Franco-Ontarian" title="Franco-Ontarian">Franco-Ontarian</a> population. As a result several <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and English words are used interchangeably. A number of phrases and expressions may also be found in Northern Ontario that are not present in the rest of the province,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> such as the use of <i>camp</i> for a summer home where Southern Ontario speakers would idiomatically use <a href="/wiki/Cottage" title="Cottage">cottage</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Informal_speech">Informal speech</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Informal speech">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <i>rubber</i> in the U.S. and Canada is slang for a condom; however, in Canada it is sometimes (rarely except for <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> and South Western Ontario) another term for an <a href="/wiki/Eraser" title="Eraser">eraser</a> (as it is in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>).
</p><p>The word <i>bum</i> can refer either to the buttocks (as in Britain), or, derogatorily, to a <a href="/wiki/Homeless" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeless">homeless</a> person (as in the U.S.). However, the "buttocks" sense does not have the indecent character it retains in British use, as it and "butt" are commonly used as a polite or childish euphemism for ruder words such as <i><a href="/wiki/Buttocks" title="Buttocks">arse</a></i> (commonly used in Atlantic Canada and among older people in Ontario and to the west) or <i><a href="/wiki/Buttocks" title="Buttocks">ass</a></i>, or <i>mitiss</i> (used in the Prairie Provinces, especially in northern and central Saskatchewan; probably originally a Cree loanword). Older Canadians may see "bum" as more polite than "butt", which before the 1980s was often considered rude.
</p><p>Similarly the word <i>pissed</i> can refer either to being drunk (as in Britain), or being angry (as in the U.S.), though anger is more often said as <i>pissed off</i>, while <i>piss drunk</i> or <i>pissed up</i> is said to describe inebriation (though <i>piss drunk</i> is sometimes also used in the US, especially in the northern states).
</p><p>One of the most distinctive Canadian phrases is the spoken interrogation or tag <i><a href="/wiki/Eh" title="Eh">eh</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The only usage of <i>eh</i> exclusive to Canada, according to the <i>Canadian Oxford Dictionary</i>, is for "ascertaining the comprehension, continued interest, agreement, etc., of the person or persons addressed" as in, "It's four kilometres away, eh, so I have to go by bike." In that case, <i>eh?</i> is used to confirm the attention of the listener and to invite a supportive noise such as <i>mm</i> or <i>oh</i> or <i>okay</i>. This usage is also common in <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. Other uses of <i>eh</i>&#160;– for instance, in place of <i>huh?</i> or <i>what?</i> meaning "please repeat or say again"&#160;– are also found in parts of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. It is common in Northern/Central <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maritimes" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritimes">Maritimes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Prairies" title="Canadian Prairies">Prairie provinces</a>. The word <i>eh</i> is used quite frequently in the North Central dialect, so a Canadian accent is often perceived in people from <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg/220px-Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg/330px-Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg/440px-Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg 2x" data-file-width="549" data-file-height="306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Curtiss_JN-4_CAN.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Canadian-built Curtiss JN-4C "Canuck" training biplane of 1918, with a differing vertical tail to the original U.S. version</div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>Canuck</i> simply means <i>Canadian</i> in its <a href="/wiki/Demonym" title="Demonym">demonymic</a> form, and, as a term used even by Canadians themselves, it is not considered derogatory. In the 19th century and early 20th century it tended to refer to <a href="/wiki/French-Canadian" class="mw-redirect" title="French-Canadian">French-Canadians</a>, while the only Canadian-built version of the popular World War I-era American <a href="/wiki/Curtiss_JN-4" class="mw-redirect" title="Curtiss JN-4">Curtiss JN-4</a> <i>Jenny</i> training biplane aircraft, the JN-4C, got the "Canuck" nickname, 1,260 of which were built. The nickname <a href="/wiki/Janey_Canuck" class="mw-redirect" title="Janey Canuck">Janey Canuck</a> was used by Anglophone women's rights writer <a href="/wiki/Emily_Murphy" title="Emily Murphy">Emily Murphy</a> in the 1920s and the <i><a href="/wiki/Johnny_Canuck" title="Johnny Canuck">Johnny Canuck</a></i> comic book character of the 1940s. Throughout the 1970s, Canada's winning World Cup men's downhill ski team was called the "<a href="/wiki/Crazy_Canucks" title="Crazy Canucks">Crazy Canucks</a>" for their fearlessness on the slopes.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> It is also the name of the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Canucks" title="Vancouver Canucks">Vancouver Canucks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> team of <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, British Columbia.
</p><p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Hoser" title="Hoser">hoser</a></i>, popularized by <a href="/wiki/Bob_%26_Doug_McKenzie" class="mw-redirect" title="Bob &amp; Doug McKenzie">Bob &amp; Doug McKenzie</a>, typically refers to an uncouth, beer-swilling male and is a euphemism for "loser" coming from the earlier days of hockey played on an outdoor rink and the losing team would have to hose down the ice after the game so it froze smooth.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Bob &amp; Doug also popularized the use of <i>Beauty, eh</i>, another western slang term which may be used to describe something as being of interest or note or deserving approval.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>A <i>Newf</i> or <i>Newfie</i> is someone from Newfoundland and Labrador; sometimes considered derogatory. In Newfoundland, the term <i>Mainlander</i> refers to any Canadian (sometimes American, occasionally Labradorian) not from the <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">island of Newfoundland</a>. <i>Mainlander</i> is also occasionally used derogatorily.
</p><p>In the Maritimes, a <i>Caper</i> or "Cape Bretoner" is someone from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island" title="Cape Breton Island">Cape Breton Island</a>, a <i>Bluenoser</i> is someone with a thick, usually southern Nova Scotia accent or as a general term for a Nova Scotian (Including Cape Bretoners), while an <i>Islander</i> is someone from <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a> (the same term is used in <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> for people from <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Island" title="Vancouver Island">Vancouver Island</a>, or the numerous islands along it). A <i>Haligonian</i> refers to someone from the city of <a href="/wiki/Halifax_Regional_Municipality" class="mw-redirect" title="Halifax Regional Municipality">Halifax</a>.
</p><p>Cape Bretoners and Newfies (from Newfoundland and Labrador) often have similar slang. "Barmp" is often used as the sound a car horn makes, example: "He cut me off so I Barmped the horn at him". When saying "B'y", while sounds like the traditional farewell, it is a syncopated shortening of the word "boy", referring to a person, example: "How's it goin, b'y?". Another slang that is commonly used is "doohickey" which means an object, example: "Pass me that doohickey over there". When an individual uses the word "biffed", they mean that they threw something. Example: "I got frustrated so I biffed it across the room".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Canadianisms">Other Canadianisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other Canadianisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>The alphanumeric code appended to mail addresses (the equivalent of the similar British <a href="/wiki/Postcode" class="mw-redirect" title="Postcode">postcode</a> and the all-numeric American <a href="/wiki/ZIP_code" class="mw-redirect" title="ZIP code">ZIP code</a>) is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Canadian_postal_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian postal code">postal code</a></i>.</li>
<li>The term <i><a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a></i> is often used in Canada to refer to what are called American Indians or <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> in the United States. This term does <em>not</em> include the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="Métis people (Canada)">Métis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>, however; the term <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal peoples">aboriginal peoples</a> (and sometimes spelled with a capital "A": "Aboriginal peoples") is preferred when all three groups are included. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Eskimo" title="Eskimo">Eskimo</a></i> has been replaced by the term Inuit in the past few decades. It is now considered offensive to use the term <i>Eskimo</i>, but is still used commonly (without pejorative intent) by those born in the early-mid-20th century.</li>
<li>"Going camping" still refers to staying in a <a href="/wiki/Tent" title="Tent">tent</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Campground" class="mw-redirect" title="Campground">campground</a> or wilderness area, while "going out to camp" may refer to a summer cottage or home in a rural area. "Going to camp" refers to children's <a href="/wiki/Summer_camp" title="Summer camp">summer camps</a>. In British Columbia, "camp" was used as a reference for certain company towns (for example, <a href="/wiki/Bridge_River,_British_Columbia" title="Bridge River, British Columbia">Bridge River</a>). It is used in western Canada to refer to logging and mining camps such as <a href="/wiki/Juskatla_Camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Juskatla Camp">Juskatla Camp</a>. It is also a synonym for a mining district; the latter occurs in names such as Camp McKinney and usages such as "Cariboo gold camp" and "Slocan mining camp" for the Cariboo goldfields and Slocan silver-galena mining district, respectively. A "cottage" in British Columbia is generally a small house, perhaps with an English design or flavour, while in southern Ontario it more likely means a <a href="/wiki/Vacation_property" class="mw-redirect" title="Vacation property">second home</a> on a lake. Similarly, "chalet" – originally a term for a small warming hut&#160;– can mean a second home of any size, but refers to one located in a ski resort. In Northern Ontario, these second homes tend to be called "camps". In Western Canada, these second homes tend to be called "cabins". A "bunkie" is a secondary building at these second homes that are small enough to require no building permits and house extra guests visiting.</li>
<li>One of the other distinctions between Canadian English and British English is the use of the phrase "try to + infinitive" versus the use of the phrase "try and + infinitive". Canadian English uses "try to" while British English uses "try and". Originally, the distinction did not exist, but through the evolution of the French term <i lang="fr" title="French language text">trier</i>, meaning 'pick out', 'separate', or 'distinguish' into the English <i>try</i>, a number of meanings were adopted along the way, including 'attempt'. Canadian English speakers use "try and" 30% of the time while British English speakers use it 73% of the time. However, since the 2000s, the two terms have begun to see equally frequent usage in British English.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>A <i>stagette</i> is a female bachelorette party (US) or hen party (UK).</li>
<li>A "shag" is thought, erroneously, to be derived from "shower" and "stag", and describes a dance where alcohol, entry tickets, raffle tickets, and so on, are sold to raise money for the engaged couple's wedding. Normally a Northwest Ontario, Northern Ontario<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and sometimes Manitoba term, a "stag and doe" or "buck and doe" is used elsewhere in Ontario. The more common term for this type of event in Manitoba is a "social".</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Humidex" title="Humidex">humidex</a></i> is a measurement used by meteorologists to reflect the combined effect of heat and <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">humidity</a> (vs. US term <i>heat index</i> quantifying the apparent temperature).</li>
<li><i>The States</i>: Commonly used to refer to the United States or almost as often <i>the U.S.</i>, much less often <i>U.S.A.</i> or <i>America</i> which are commonly used in other countries, the latter more often used in other English-speaking nations.</li>
<li><i>Drop the gloves</i>: to begin a fight. A reference to a practice in hockey of removing gloves prior to fighting, and the idiom "throw down the gauntlet".</li>
<li><i>Back east</i> typically means 'Ontario or possibly Quebec' whereas <i>Down East</i> instead refers to the Maritimes<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> – the latter term is used in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, especially in areas very close to <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Canada" title="Atlantic Canada">Atlantic Canada</a>, to refer to <a href="/wiki/Maine_accent#Lexicon" title="Maine accent">the two eastern coastal counties of Maine</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitudes_towards_Canadian_English">Attitudes towards Canadian English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Attitudes towards Canadian English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2011, just under 21.5&#160;million Canadians, representing 65% of the population, spoke English most of the time at home, while 58% declared it their mother language.<sup id="cite_ref-pop1_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pop1-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> English is the major language everywhere in Canada except Quebec, and most Canadians (85%) can speak English.<sup id="cite_ref-Navigator_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Navigator-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> While English is not the preferred language in Quebec, 36.1% of Québécois can speak English.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Nationally, <a href="/wiki/Francophone" class="mw-redirect" title="Francophone">Francophones</a> are five times more likely to speak English than Anglophones are to speak French – 44% and 9% respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Only 3.2% of Canada's English-speaking population resides in Quebec—mostly in Montreal.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Attitude studies on Canadian English are somewhat rare. A perceptual study on Albertan and Ontarians exists<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> in combination with older literature from the 1970s–80s. Sporadic reports can be found in the literature, e.g. on Vancouver English,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> in which more than 80% believe in a "Canadian way of speaking", with those with a university education reporting higher than those without.
</p><p>Jaan Lilles argues in an essay for <i>English Today</i> that there is no variety of "Canadian English". He acknowledges that no variety of English is more "real" or "natural" than any other, but that, in the words of American linguist John Algeo, "All linguistic varieties are fictions." According to Lilles, Canadian English is simply not a "useful fiction".<sup id="cite_ref-Lilles_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilles-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> He goes on to argue that too often national identity is conflated with linguistic identity, and that in the case of "Canadian English", supposedly unique features of Canadian speakers, such as certain lexical terms such as <i>muskeg</i> are artificially exaggerated to distinguish Canadian speech primarily from that found in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Lilles_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilles-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Canadian_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-ethn-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ethn_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ethnologue.com/18/language/eng/">English (Canada)</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> (18th ed., 2015)</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-wikidata-6a3de803f75f2a358711fe275430f5a7a7fcbaf3-v3-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-wikidata-6a3de803f75f2a358711fe275430f5a7a7fcbaf3-v3_4-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.iana.org/assignments/language-subtag-registry/language-subtag-registry">"Canada"</a>; <i>IANA language subtag registry</i>; named as: CA; publication date: 16 October 2005; retrieved: 11 January 2019.</span>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><code>en-CA</code> is the <a href="/wiki/Language_code" title="Language code">language code</a> for <i>Canadian English</i>, as defined by <a href="/wiki/ISO_standard" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO standard">ISO standards</a> (see <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-1" title="ISO 639-1">ISO 639-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-1_alpha-2" title="ISO 3166-1 alpha-2">ISO 3166-1 alpha-2</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Internet_standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet standard">Internet standards</a> (see <a href="/wiki/IETF_language_tag" title="IETF language tag">IETF language tag</a>).</span>
</li>
